  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app',

<span id="freeTextContainer16734572820629006511">When Pierre-Anthon realizes there is no meaning to life, the seventh-grader leaves his classroom, climbs a tree, and stays there. His classmates cannot make him come down, not even by pelting him with rocks. So to prove to Pierre-Anthon that life has meaning, the children decide to give up things of importance.   <span id="freeText16734572820629006511" style="display:none">When Pierre-Anthon realizes there is no meaning to life, the seventh-grader leaves his classroom, climbs a tree, and stays there. His classmates cannot make him come down, not even by pelting him with rocks. So to prove to Pierre-Anthon that life has meaning, the children decide to give up things of importance. The pile starts with the superficial—a fishing rod, a new pair of shoes. But as the sacrifices become more extreme, the students grow increasingly desperate to get Pierre-Anthon down, to justify their belief in meaning. Sure to prompt intense thought and discussion, <i>Nothing</i>—already a treasured work overseas—is not to be missed.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/472107-help-i-feel-stupid-silly-not">Help! I feel stupid silly not understanding the ending of the book, in fact all of the book. Does anyone know what the meaning is? I&#39;m so confused, any clarification would be greatly appreciated.</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_472107'><a id="like_count_community_question_472107" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/472107?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">4 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F6647312-nothing">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/472107-help-i-feel-stupid-silly-not">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/472107-help-i-feel-stupid-silly-not">See all 4 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/48398380-chris-mayes"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1446149576i/48398380._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/48398380-chris-mayes">Chris Mayes</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Frame the question this way:<br /><br />Why should anyone keep on living? Why not kill yourself and escape all the problems in life? You might say "well because d<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Frame the question this way:<br /><br />Why should anyone keep on living? Why not kill yourself and escape all the problems in life? You might say "well because death is unpleasant," but if the only thing nice to say about life is that it beats dying, that doesn't say anything very nice about life!<br /><br />So, the search for "meaning" is a search for a reason to keep on living. Perhaps your beloved pet gets you out of bed in the morning, or maybe it's your hope of eventually finding a loving spouse and a happy family, or perhaps then again you think you ought to keep living because God demands that. These are all "meanings" for life.<br /><br />Ok, now apply that to the book. A young man (Pierre Anthon) decides that there is no "meaning"; we should just give up on life and sit around waiting for death. His classmates, in response, come up with all their reasons for living--all the things that make their lives valuable.<br /><br />The joke at the end, however, is that they were willing to give those things up just to impress Pierre Anthon (and the rest of the world). And if they're willing to give them up just for some money and temporary approval from people they don't know, it can't really be that meaningful, right? The search for meaning, ironically, destroys much of what was meaningful in the characters' lives, because the meaning didn't exist *in the things* but *in the characters' relationship to the things*. <br /><br />So, the kids kill Pierre Anthon--and, I would argue, they also attack the very idea of trying to "prove" meaning to other people. Better, the author seems to argue, not to "mess with" the meaning, to keep it private and close to your heart. Expose it too much to social games, and you'll end up selling your soul.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_99072499" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/99072499" />
    <a title="karen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45618-karen"><img alt="karen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596291582p2/45618.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/99072499?book_show_action=true">Apr 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="karen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="karen" href="/user/show/45618-karen">karen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to karen by: </span>
      ariel
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=thanks-for-prezzies">thanks-for-prezzies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=why-yes-i-ya">why-yes-i-ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=creepy-children">creepy-children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer99072499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1446896075107805695">the beginning part of this book reminded me of my very favorite part in john gardner's <i>grendel</i>, where two characters are ass-kickingly fighting, but also having a philosophical debate at the same time. this starts out like that, only with less howling. this is teen fiction, so the weapons being pelted are plums, and everyone gets to keep their arms (for now), but that doesn't mean this is sweet valley high: there will be blood and sacrifice and deep dark nihilism in these 13-year-olds before we </span>
  <span id="freeText1446896075107805695" style="display:none">the beginning part of this book reminded me of my very favorite part in john gardner's <i>grendel</i>, where two characters are ass-kickingly fighting, but also having a philosophical debate at the same time. this starts out like that, only with less howling. this is teen fiction, so the weapons being pelted are plums, and everyone gets to keep their arms (for now), but that doesn't mean this is sweet valley high: there will be blood and sacrifice and deep dark nihilism in these 13-year-olds before we are through.<br><br>this is my favorite type of story, where kids revert to savagery when they are put into an isolating situation. in this case, it is emotional isolation rather than physical, <i>lord of the flies</i> style, but it is serious.<br><br>quick plot summary tho i hate to do it: 7th grade class, one kid "realizes" that nothing means anything and so climbs a tree and stays there for months, shouting his newfound beliefs and infuriating the rest of the kids in his class. they decide to prove to him that life has meaning. they begin to accumulate a "pile of meaning", willingly giving up what matters to them in order to prove him wrong, because he is starting to get into their heads and cause doubt. but who wants to give up the thing that matters most to them, at thirteen? and what kid is going to allow his classmate to cheat and give up something lesser, while hoarding their treasures? so they begin choosing for each other, forcing others to give up increasingly meaningful parts of themselves. and now it's a party, and it's going to get messy.<br><br>most authors would stop here - deliver a little cautionary allegory all tidied up. but there is a little more to it here. a little more of the dark. <br><br>ariel has recommended a tremendous book to me, and now i pass it along to you.<br><br><br>for fun, google image "pile of meaning" with your kiddie filters turned off. oh, my...<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>

<div id="review_996933965" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/996933965" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/996933965?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2014">2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer996933965" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16918974752518788046"><b>
  <i>"You'll find out you're a clown in a trivial circus where everyone tries to convince each other how vital it is to have a certain look one year and another the next. And then you'll find out that fame and the big wide world are outside of you, and that inside there's nothing, and always will be, no matter what you do."</i>
</b><br/><br/>I have been <u>saving</u> this book for years. It's one of those books that had enough glowing reviews and literary accolades to make me almost certain I would like it. Not only that,</span>
  <span id="freeText16918974752518788046" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"You'll find out you're a clown in a trivial circus where everyone tries to convince each other how vital it is to have a certain look one year and another the next. And then you'll find out that fame and the big wide world are outside of you, and that inside there's nothing, and always will be, no matter what you do."</i>
</b><br /><br />I have been <u>saving</u> this book for years. It's one of those books that had enough glowing reviews and literary accolades to make me almost certain I would like it. Not only that, but it is about the subject of existential nihilism - which, frankly, <i>fascinates</i> me and has for a long time. I'm rather inclined to believe the world is meaningless; or at the very least has a certain abstract meaning that is defined by individual perspective and experience. So, really, <i>Nothing</i> had me at the premise. But it just didn't deliver for me.<br /><br />I can see some of the attraction - it's a complex book that once again proves YA doesn't have to be shallow or lacking in "literary value". It demands that you step outside of your normal mode of thinking and ask yourself questions: <b>is everything pointless? Can meaning be found anywhere? If nothing matters, is it better to just do nothing?</b><br /><br />It all starts when Pierre Anthon stands up in the classroom one day and declares "Nothing matters. I have known that for a long time. So nothing is worth doing. I just realized that." He then parks himself up in a plum tree outside the school, refuses to come down, and spouts constant odes to the meaninglessness of life, the universe and everything. His classmates grow increasingly uncomfortable with what Pierre says, so they decide to gather a "heap of meaning" - a pile of what is most precious to them, in order to prove that certain things do have value. <br /><br />As they are required to give up more and more of what is important to them, soon they start to turn on one another and the sacrifices become ever more extreme. It is this part of the book that I personally found most effective: the gradual disappearance of morality and the way the children turn to violence. Despite the simplistic sentence structure (a possible side effect of the translation), this is a very mature piece of YA that contains many disturbing scenes.<br /><br />What I didn't like began with the short, choppy sentences and continued to grow worse with the complete lack of realism in the story. <i>Nothing</i> feels more like a philosophical essay than a novel. I never developed a connection with any of the characters, nor any sympathy for them - not even the narrator. And there was no way I could believe that these young teens were allowed to run about digging up graves and stealing from science labs over the space of several months without some adult questioning what the hell was going on. <br /><br />I understand that this novel is primarily intended to provoke philosophical thinking, but I believe it would have been far more effective if we were allowed to warm to someone in the novel and develop an emotional connection with them - something I personally feel was lacking. And there was another thing I didn't like. I don't want to give away any spoilers, but certain characters equated female virginity and innocence with the self. As in: if you lose your virginity, you also lose yourself, which is ridiculous.<br /><br />And maybe we should call it irony or something, but this whole book felt a little pointless. <b>In the end, it seems I took nothing from it.</b> At first I actually wondered if that was the whole point - serves you right, Emily, for spending a couple of quid on <i>Nothing</i>! Ha-Ha, you fool. It would've been quite cool - if somewhat infuriating - if the book had delivered its promise and carried no message because there is no message because everything is meaningless... but no, I think there was something we were supposed to get here that was obviously lost on me.<br /><br /><a href="http://thebookgeek.co.uk" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.leafmarks.com/emilymay" rel="nofollow noopener">Leafmarks</a> | <a href="https://www.facebook.com/thebookgeekreviews" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/thebookgeek01" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | <a href="http://instagram.com/emleemay" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="http://mycountryheart.co.uk/" rel="nofollow noopener">Tumblr</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16918974752518788046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer160957597" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11074969698222391588">just this week -- i was riding the bus on my way to school. and as i was sitting there and pondering about life (aren't i the intellectual?), i noticed this guy who was sitting in the row in front of me, who was doing something with his hands. he couldn't seem to stop his nervous gestures. so i looked. (of course i looked.)<br/><br/>dude was scratching on practically every exposed skin surface on his body. he generously liberated his ears from earwax. picked his nose as if there was no tomorrow. rubbed hi</span>
  <span id="freeText11074969698222391588" style="display:none">just this week -- i was riding the bus on my way to school. and as i was sitting there and pondering about life (aren't i the intellectual?), i noticed this guy who was sitting in the row in front of me, who was doing something with his hands. he couldn't seem to stop his nervous gestures. so i looked. (of course i looked.)<br /><br />dude was scratching on practically every exposed skin surface on his body. he generously liberated his ears from earwax. picked his nose as if there was no tomorrow. rubbed his eyes till they were red. ran his fingers through his hair, yanking some in the process. a minute ticked away. ten minutes. a solid hour passed. he <b>did not</b> stop. and i tried desperately <i>not</i> to see anything out of the corner of my eyes. <br /><br />so, this is what <b>nothing</b> <s>is</s> feels like to me. you can't help but stare, no matter how disturbing. try as you might to distance yourself from things that make you uncomfortable, but they are still happening. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11074969698222391588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer474323263" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer447483196643119850">ZERO STARS..... (less than ZERO)! <br/><br/>Shame on this author! This book is creepy-creepy-creepy!<br/><br/>Disturbing and irritating. <br/><br/>NOTE: I would NEVER suggest this book for ANYONE of ANY age to read. ---<br/>I read a few reviews who wrote SPOIL ALERT before writing more of what they had to say. However, 'nothing' they could say would spoil this book anymore than its ALREADY SPOILED. I could tell you every little ugly-nasty-yucky detail about this book ---in 5 minutes if you are really THAT curious ---(ask me if yo</span>
  <span id="freeText447483196643119850" style="display:none">ZERO STARS..... (less than ZERO)! <br /><br />Shame on this author! This book is creepy-creepy-creepy!<br /><br />Disturbing and irritating. <br /><br />NOTE: I would NEVER suggest this book for ANYONE of ANY age to read. ---<br />I read a few reviews who wrote SPOIL ALERT before writing more of what they had to say. However, 'nothing' they could say would spoil this book anymore than its ALREADY SPOILED. I could tell you every little ugly-nasty-yucky detail about this book ---in 5 minutes if you are really THAT curious ---(ask me if you need to know) ---but don't waste your time -or money supporting this book. That is MY STRONG OPINION! (I know I'm just one person here---but I feel VERY STRONG about this). <br /><br />I have no problem with the 'concept' the philosophical question of whether life has meaning or not ---(by 7th grade kids) --- I think its a little silly for adults to debate this question. ---<br />but this book can't handle this 'question' without manipulation. (shock you to keep reading???) ----YUCK YUCK YUCK! ---<br /><br />but this book offers NOTHING of VALUE!!! The BEST part of the book is the title: *NOTHING*!!!!<br /><br /><br />REALLY---the author should be ashamed of herself. This is her best writing contribution??? I'm ANGRY about this book. I'm angry its getting 'awards' --- (something is very wrong in our world if this book is getting awards). <br /><br />I would NOT want my 7th grade child to read it. Its WAY to violent. (also, no consequences for the crimes of these kids). ---And ---as an adult--I do not need this book either! <br /><br />WHY??? WHY?? WHY??? do we need books like this TODAY....with all the 'already' horrific brutal killings of 'children' in the news today? <br /><br />This book makes NO positive difference AT ALL!!! <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_201035784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201035784" />
    <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers"><img alt="TheBookSmugglers" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316717753p2/1223249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201035784?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="TheBookSmugglers" class="user" itemprop="url" name="TheBookSmugglers" href="/user/show/1223249-thebooksmugglers">TheBookSmugglers</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201035784" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7793968429087281316">Original review posted on The Book Smugglers <a href='http://thebooksmugglers.com/2011/08/book-review-nothing-by-janne-teller.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>HERE</a><br/><br/>Warning! This review contains spoilers! Do not read if you don’t want to know what happens! You have been warned!<br/><br/>Nothing is an award-winning book (including an ALA Printz Honor) and has received tons of very positive reviews but it wasn’t until John Green raved about it on Twitter a few months ago that I decided to buy it. A few weeks ago in the middle of a reading slump, I looked at the book, it looked back at me, and I thought surely this is a s</span>
  <span id="freeText7793968429087281316" style="display:none">Original review posted on The Book Smugglers <a href="http://thebooksmugglers.com/2011/08/book-review-nothing-by-janne-teller.html" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a><br /><br />Warning! This review contains spoilers! Do not read if you don’t want to know what happens! You have been warned!<br /><br />Nothing is an award-winning book (including an ALA Printz Honor) and has received tons of very positive reviews but it wasn’t until John Green raved about it on Twitter a few months ago that I decided to buy it. A few weeks ago in the middle of a reading slump, I looked at the book, it looked back at me, and I thought surely this is a safe bet, all things considered. Well, that only goes to show how much reading is like a box of chocolates: you never know what you going to get. I didn’t like this book at all.<br /><br />The story is set in a small town in Denmark and it starts this one day when 13-year-old Pierre Anthon stands up in the middle of the classroom and says:<br /><br /> Nothing matters.<br /> I have known that for a long time.<br /> So nothing is worth doing.<br /> I just realised that.<br /><br />He then leaves school to sit in a plum tree from where he taunts the other students day after day with things like:<br /><br /> Everything begins just in order to end. The moment you were born you began to die, and that goes for everything else as well.<br /><br />The students grow uneasy about what Pierre is telling them. What if Pierre is right and nothing has meaning? So his classmates decide to show him that things do mean something and set out to build a pile of meaning to show him. They start working at this closed mill, where they start building said pile by adding things that matter to them, willingly giving them up. They start with simple things, like someone’s favourite shoes or someone’s new bike. But then they realise that these are not meaningful enough.<br /><br />They also realise that one does not have the strength to give up more meaningful things so they decide to choose on behalf of each other and they start to demand more in a horrifying crescendo: they dig out the body of a dead brother and add the coffin with its contents to the pile and they add this kids’ beloved pet. The stakes are amplified with every subsequent pick: a devout Muslim’s praying rug, a Christ in the cross, one girl’s innocence (she is raped by some of the boys, who put a rag with her blood and their semen in the pile), a guitarist’s finger.<br /><br />This goes on for months and months until eventually one of the kids breaks down and tells the adults. The media creates a circus around it, although the kids suffer no real consequences after all they’ve done. A museum declares the pile to be Art, and therefore the pile does have meaning. The kids agree to sell it for millions of dollars. And just then Pierre finally gets around seeing the pile they built for him and promptly declares it meaningless – because they sold it so easily. The kids get really fed up at this point, and they beat Pierre to death, set the barn and his body on fire to make it seem an accident and they go on their merry lives. The end.<br /><br />I will just start by saying that I have no problem with the fact that the book is bleak, violent and works as an expression of extreme nihilism. That’s not the reason I disliked it. I also have no problem with the philosophical questioning: the question of whether life has meaning or not is an important question to be asked even though I am not personally fond of existential nihilism.<br /><br />No, the reason why I disliked the book is simple: I don’t think it is a good novel. I think it is flawed in terms of executing its basic premise, the development of the story is contrived and manipulative and the writing is irritating to say the least.<br /><br />The reader is supposed to accept that a bunch of 7th graders gets around their really small town over a long period of time (months and months) doing these things completely unnoticed and unchecked by their parents, neighbours and teachers because one kid is up in a tree yelling things at them? I am supposed to suspend disbelief and accept the premise that this kid just sits up on this tree day after day after day and no one does anything – where are his parents? Am I supposed to accept that every single student is equally distressed by what Pierre tells them and that not a single one of them offers a dissenting voice?<br /><br />I am supposed to accept all of that as part of the philosophical aspects of the novel. But I can’t: this is NOT a philosophical essay; it is supposed to be a work of fiction, set in our time and in a really small town. This is not speculative fiction, and there’s nothing that is even remotely fantastic about this story which could help with suspension of disbelief: it is not a fairytale; it is not set in the future or in a dystopian society. I am sure that the point is to show how group mentality works and how searching for the meaning of life could happen anywhere, anytime but come on, these kids are not isolated (and therefore the comparisons with Lord of the Flies are not exactly apropos), life goes on, they go to school every day, they go home every day. I don’t buy how this could have happened. So therefore, in terms of a fictional story it does not stand against close scrutiny.<br /><br />Furthermore: some things really bothered me. There are no consequences for the crimes (yes, they are crimes) committed by the kids. Apparently only one of them has to face his parents and it is the Muslim kid – who is severely beaten up. After all of that happens, only one of them completely loses it and goes insane in a very sociopathic way: the girl who was raped and lost her virginity. Because apparently you can get over everything in this life and you don’t really lose your innocence if you do despicable things, you only lose it if you lose your virginity. I am not saying that she shouldn’t have severe emotional problems after the terrible thing that has been done to her, but I do find it problematic that she is the only one. But who knows, maybe I am missing some philosophical point here?<br /><br />But because of all that, I felt the development of the story and the amplification of the tension to be extremely contrived, and felt as though the events were being manipulated to fit the philosophical premise. It never felt like a natural crescendo.<br /><br />Finally, there is the writing. Its minimalist writing, full of short, staccato sentences was extremely grating and they happen every 2-3 pages. I understand this is a translation from Danish – maybe this sort of writing sounds better in the original language? Examples:<br /><br /> All of a sudden I was scared. Scared of Pierre Anthon.<br /> Scared, more scared, most scared.<br /><br /> We’d win!<br /> Victory is sweet.Victory is. Victory<br /><br /> We didn’t reply. Not one of us. Five-zero-two<br /><br /> He was going to see right through us.<br /> Squat. Zilch. Nothing.<br /><br /> Then I noticed how quiet it was in the mill.<br /> Quiet. Quieter. All quiet.<br /><br />So yeah, Nothing did not rock my boat and I know I am the minority when it comes to this one (on top of awards, it also got starred reviews from Booklist, Kirkus and Publishers Weekly). But, hey what the hell do I know? I quoted Forrest Gump in my intro to this review. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7793968429087281316" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3382840026" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3382840026" />
    <a title="Elena May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57120721-elena-may"><img alt="Elena May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581020130p2/57120721.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3382840026?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elena May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elena May" href="/user/show/57120721-elena-may">Elena May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3382840026" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5939333947807413388">An excellent, brutal, smart, nihilistic, philosophical coming-of-age story, in which a group of seven-graders search for meaning. But first, they must lose so much.<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>“And although we'd sworn we'd never become like them, that was exactly what was happening. We weren't even fifteen yet.<br/>Thirteen, fourteen, adult, dead.”</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>Since I recently moved to Denmark, I’ve been thinking I should start reading more Danish fiction. As a child, I used to read lots of Scandinavian literature, but for some r</span>
  <span id="freeText5939333947807413388" style="display:none">An excellent, brutal, smart, nihilistic, philosophical coming-of-age story, in which a group of seven-graders search for meaning. But first, they must lose so much.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>“And although we'd sworn we'd never become like them, that was exactly what was happening. We weren't even fifteen yet.<br />Thirteen, fourteen, adult, dead.”</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />Since I recently moved to Denmark, I’ve been thinking I should start reading more Danish fiction. As a child, I used to read lots of Scandinavian literature, but for some reason drifted away from those books. And this creepy little novella is a great way to get back on track!<br /><br />In a small fictional Danish town, which looks no different from real small Danish towns all around, a boy suddenly decides nothing has a meaning. He stops going to school and spends his days sitting in a tree, shouting at his classmates about the meaninglessness of it all.<br /><br />Naturally, his classmates have to prove him wrong. And in their youthful curiosity, they plunge into a quest to discover the <i>meaning</i>. How? By collecting objects and symbols that mean something to someone, piling them on and on in an abandoned sawmill in an evergrowing heap. The Heap of Meaning.<br /><br />Only, for the heap to have a meaning, they need to give up objects that really <i>mean</i> something to them. They don’t trust each other to willingly make a sacrifice, and so each child chooses what the next one will give up. The requests quickly escalate in importance and gruesomeness, and while initially many push back, at the end the group forces everyone to comply and give up more and more.<br /><br /><b>I have only one criticism: </b>The children are supposed to be 14, but they sounded younger. Just the way they were thinking, speaking, interacting, and the things they were doing, it sounded to me like something younger children would say or do.<br /><br />Other than that, the book felt very real. I’ve seen some reviewers complain it was unrealistic that in a small town children would spend months collecting gruesome objects and no one would notice. Those statements surprised me. Children do have secret lives and get away with a lot of stuff all the time. Just thinking of all the mischief and complex schemes we’ve been doing as kids, with no adults finding out, and a small town is an ideal place for this to happen. For me, the children’s secret plots felt very true.<br /><br />Apart from my thoughts on the age, the author did a wonderful job capturing children’s minds. Children can be cruel, vindictive, and not thinking of consequences. The purpose of the heap of meaning is quickly twisted, everyone aiming to hurt. And they can also be kind and supportive, and curious. Always searching, finding meaning in things adults would not always understand. The kids we meet here are not nice, but they are real.<br /><br />But then, they have to grow up:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>
    <b>“We cried because we had lost something and gained something else. And because it hurt both losing and gaining. And because we knew what we had lost but weren't as yet able to put into words what it was we had gained.”</b>
  </i>
</blockquote><br /><br />The book asks so many questions. Does anything have meaning to anyone? Treasured objects, memories, religion, love for one’s country, innocence, abilities? Do these things lose their value if we share them? If we sell them for money? And if they can lose this meaning, did they <i>ever</i> have meaning at all? The children must learn harsh lessons, and in the process become the adults they never wanted to be. Losing the meaning is a rite of passage, towards the search for new meanings.<br /><br />On a separate note, while this story could have happened nearly anywhere, I enjoyed it how now that I’m more familiar with Danish society, I kept recognizing the typically Danish things. The style of names and nicknames, the high divorce rates, the obsessions with the flag that has supposedly descended from heaven. I’ll definitely check the author’s other works.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5939333947807413388" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_101702442" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101702442" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/42508-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368721201p2/42508.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101702442?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/42508-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42508-greg?shelf=life-is-shit">life-is-shit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42508-greg?shelf=books-for-kids">books-for-kids</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42508-greg?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42508-greg?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/42508-greg?shelf=girls-girls-girls">girls-girls-girls</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101702442" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8043375786387666013">On the first day of seventh grade one kid realizes that nothing matters. He stands up, leaves and starts spending his days sitting in a plum tree and jeering at his former classmates about the meaningless of everything. <br/><br/>His classmates are not happy with him. <br/><br/>Displeased. Angry. Furious. <br/><br/>The plot sounds kind of like Calvino's <i>Baron in the Trees</i>, another tale about a boy in a tree that annoys people just by his being in the tree. The kid in <i>Nothing</i> doesn't live in the tree though, he just spends hi</span>
  <span id="freeText8043375786387666013" style="display:none">On the first day of seventh grade one kid realizes that nothing matters. He stands up, leaves and starts spending his days sitting in a plum tree and jeering at his former classmates about the meaningless of everything. <br /><br />His classmates are not happy with him. <br /><br />Displeased. Angry. Furious. <br /><br />The plot sounds kind of like Calvino's <i>Baron in the Trees</i>, another tale about a boy in a tree that annoys people just by his being in the tree. The kid in <i>Nothing</i> doesn't live in the tree though, he just spends his days there. But he does spend it with, say I use this word?, evangelical spirit of letting other people in on the truth he has found. <br /><br />The jacket copy and some reviews talk about this book as being like <i>Lord of the Flies</i>. It's not. Except that kids do revert to a sort of savagery, but it's a different kind of savagery in place here. <i>Lord of the Flies</i> is about the flimsy constructs of civilization and how easily it is to Fall into a 'natural' state of savagery. It's like a big fuck you (opps I just cursed in a children's review) to Rousseau and his noble savage. <br /><br />When these kids go all 'savage' their fury isn't because they have stepped (been thrown, to appropriate a Heideggarian term, which is ok since this novel is existential in many ways) to a point past 'civilization'; to a malaise that could be appropriately called post-survival: the idea that life is meaningless and what one does when they are confronted with this proposition. <br /><br />This is like Plato's allegory of the cave, but the dude who escapes and comes back to spread his 'gospel' isn't saying that the world you are seeing is all an illusion but there is a better, more pure world out there; rather this world is all an illusion and there is nothing beyond it. There is no meaning to any of this, and what 'this' is isn't worth anything either. <br /><br />Anyway all of this philosophical bullshit aside, this book is much bleaker than <i>Lord of the Flies</i>. It's another one of these YA books that surprises me with its edginess (jeez I hate that term, but I'm getting sick with myself for all the putting words in quotes that is going on in his review, this one should have quotes around it too, but now I can have my ironic cake and eat it too), and for the quality of the book. <br /><br />A great dark book about meaninglessness, what people do when they are confronted with the idea that maybe there is no meaning, and through the different children many of the ways that this question is side-stepped/answered by people in our society are shown to be empty shells of delusion. <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8043375786387666013" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_140120776" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/140120776" />
    <a title="Christie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1355394-christie"><img alt="Christie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1254754752p2/1355394.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/140120776?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christie" href="/user/show/1355394-christie">Christie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer140120776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2872938642762585460">What begins as a premise with so much potential ends as a book full of nothing, or worse, an appalling disregard for the humanity of teenagers and a deeply cynical and pathological view of life. One 7th grader decides nothing is worth it and climbs a tree. The best the others can muster is to pelt rocks his way. Really?!? Why doesn't one climb the tree and join him? Where are his parents? Why is it that all kids are equally threatened by this act, which I feel would only pose a minor threat to a</span>
  <span id="freeText2872938642762585460" style="display:none">What begins as a premise with so much potential ends as a book full of nothing, or worse, an appalling disregard for the humanity of teenagers and a deeply cynical and pathological view of life. One 7th grader decides nothing is worth it and climbs a tree. The best the others can muster is to pelt rocks his way. Really?!? Why doesn't one climb the tree and join him? Where are his parents? Why is it that all kids are equally threatened by this act, which I feel would only pose a minor threat to a few, and because of that they all turn into animals driven by base instincts? They are not isolated on some remote island. They all go home every evening to their parents, so the reader is left to believe that all of their parents are deaf to the world of their kids, and no conversations about "meaning in life" or active engagement in meaningful life activities are to be had by any of them. <br /><br />One boy is a devout Muslim and he concedes to giving up his prayer mat to the "pile of meaning" and gets beaten to a pulp by his father to prove it has meaning? Really?!? A Beatles fan and avid guitar player sacrifices a finger on his hand. What?!? A girl agrees, in a very detached manner, to give up her virginity?!? Presumably this story is meant to work as a fable, but it doesn't whatsoever, and it is the sickest yarn I have yet come across in young adult literature. I find the entire story to be the most insulting diatribe against youth. <br /><br />I have heard people say, in defense of this multiple award-winning story, that it provokes discussion. Any story that provokes a worthwhile discussion has some nugget for the reader to chew on. This one has absolutely nothing, as the title implies. To me this is a gimmick of the worst kind.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2872938642762585460" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_156589196" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156589196" />
    <a title="Sesana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5172893-sesana"><img alt="Sesana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413088169p2/5172893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156589196?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sesana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sesana" href="/user/show/5172893-sesana">Sesana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172893-sesana?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5172893-sesana?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156589196" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6153486470275265485">When picking up Nothing, a reader needs to be prepared to suspend their disbelief an extraordinary amount for what is meant to be a realistic, contemporary story. A young (13-14) boy decides that nothing means anything, so he decides to spend his days in a tree yelling what are, in translation, irritating slogans about how nothing means anything. His parents and teachers? Apparently content to let him stay throughout the winter. His classmates? Actually deeply bothered by him, instead of finding</span>
  <span id="freeText6153486470275265485" style="display:none">When picking up Nothing, a reader needs to be prepared to suspend their disbelief an extraordinary amount for what is meant to be a realistic, contemporary story. A young (13-14) boy decides that nothing means anything, so he decides to spend his days in a tree yelling what are, in translation, irritating slogans about how nothing means anything. His parents and teachers? Apparently content to let him stay throughout the winter. His classmates? Actually deeply bothered by him, instead of finding him simply weird and annoying. So deeply bothered that they decide to gather things that do mean something, so they can prove he's wrong. You need to be able to accept all of that to even have a hope of making it through the story.<br /><br />I finished the book, knowing that the plot was absurd and unrealistic in the extreme, mostly out of morbid fascination. It starts small, with one teen demanding a greater sacrifice from another. And so on, and so on, in a bizarre and grotesque display of one-upsmanship. It's very, very difficult to get through. The end result of all this is... nothing. The pile of meaning gains them nothing, costs them much, and has alarmingly few consequences. I'm not even sure what the author was trying to say about meaning. Maybe all she was trying to say is that children (people?) can do some very nasty things to each other.<br /><br />The style used here didn't work for me. It was broken, oddly repetitive, and detached. (An example, describing a friend's blue hair: "Blue. Bluer. Bluest." A pattern repeated far too often.) But this is a translation. How much of this is the original author, and how much the translator? And does it work better in the original Danish? I couldn't say.<br /><br />Nothing does have the saving grace of being a short, quick read. It's a small, thin book, with lots of white space, and morbid fascination can really increase my page count. But in the end, that morbid fascination was all I got out of it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6153486470275265485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_149598262" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149598262" />
    <a title="Lawral" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/331512-lawral"><img alt="Lawral" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1188605068p2/331512.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149598262?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawral" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawral" href="/user/show/331512-lawral">Lawral</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/331512-lawral?shelf=read-but-unowned">read-but-unowned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/331512-lawral?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/331512-lawral?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149598262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer173817274532148154">Disturbing does not even begin to cover it.<br/><br/>Nothing is a tiny book. It's shorter than most and more narrow. The story takes up slightly more than 200 pages, and those pages contain a lot of white space. Still, it is probably the most disturbing book I've ever read. And almost not even in a good way. Don't get me wrong, Nothing is a wonderfully written book. Not a single word is superfluous and yet the story feels expansive. We see the whole thing from Agnes' point of view, and yet the feelings of</span>
  <span id="freeText173817274532148154" style="display:none">Disturbing does not even begin to cover it.<br /><br />Nothing is a tiny book. It's shorter than most and more narrow. The story takes up slightly more than 200 pages, and those pages contain a lot of white space. Still, it is probably the most disturbing book I've ever read. And almost not even in a good way. Don't get me wrong, Nothing is a wonderfully written book. Not a single word is superfluous and yet the story feels expansive. We see the whole thing from Agnes' point of view, and yet the feelings of others and the crowd mentality of the group are clear. It's got a kind of terrible, terrifying beauty to it. <br /><br />As Agnes and her classmates try to collect things to counter Pierre Anthon's nothingness, things take a definite turn towards the sinister. If they're going to prove meaning, these things must really mean something to the person who has to give them up. And each time someone has to give something up, they get to choose what the next person has to lose. This accumulation of things starts out as mean and a bit vindictive, but it very quickly spirals out of control until it is not just things that are being accumulated. Friendships break up, kids get in trouble, alliances are formed, and people get both emotionally and physically hurt.<br /><br />Watching what these kids require of their friends and classmates, what they deam worthy sacrifices to the "heap of meaning," was like driving past a multiple car pile-up on the freeway. It's gruesome and terrible, but you can't help but look. I finished this book in a single day, holding my hand over my gaping mouth for the last 50 pages or so (and more than a few times before that as well). I was repulsed and hooked at the same time. This is an engrossing and haunting read.<br /><br /><br />Book source: Philly Free Library</span>
  <a data-text-id="173817274532148154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/125019004" />
    <a title="Tatiana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana"><img alt="Tatiana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1249783659p2/2250020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/125019004?book_show_action=true">Oct 06, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tatiana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tatiana" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
      </span>

          added it




          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Tatiana by: </span>
      Franny
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=children-s-middle-school">children-s-middle-school</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=printz">printz</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer125019004" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13098525705730022979">I am at a loss here and can't really get myself to choose how to rate this book. It certainly provoked an array of emotions in me, but I find it hard to articulate what I thought of it as a whole. Which is not that surprising considering that philosophical books about meaning/meaninglessness of life never work for me. I sort of already have that question answered for myself and no one's nihilistic ideas can change it (I hope). Kids in this novel, however, are easily influenced by such ideas and </span>
  <span id="freeText13098525705730022979" style="display:none">I am at a loss here and can't really get myself to choose how to rate this book. It certainly provoked an array of emotions in me, but I find it hard to articulate what I thought of it as a whole. Which is not that surprising considering that philosophical books about meaning/meaninglessness of life never work for me. I sort of already have that question answered for myself and no one's nihilistic ideas can change it (I hope). Kids in this novel, however, are easily influenced by such ideas and fight back the doubts by creating the pile of meaning, to which they sacrifice things most important to them. What do they have left then? <br /><br />The other aspect of the novel - the herd mentality and savagery humans are capable of - is easier to be affected by. <br /><br />What I am certain about is that this very allegorical fable will not be a hit with its target audience - middle school kids. Some adults will appreciate it, and for a reason.<br /><br />I wonder what Teller writes for adults of this is what she wrote for kids...</span>
  <a data-text-id="13098525705730022979" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_236047191" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Minli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/981514-minli"><img alt="Minli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319807416p2/981514.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/236047191?book_show_action=true">Nov 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Minli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Minli" href="/user/show/981514-minli">Minli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=inked-2012">inked-2012</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer236047191" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9745975458330583730">Bleh. Reading <i>Nothing</i> is like watching a train wreck you know is coming. You know it's going to be depressing as hell, but just you can't look away. <br/><br/>I will not deny that the prose--as well as the translation--is excellent. The novel was immensely readable and despite all my insides cringing I had to know what happened next. <br/><br/>What I simply don't buy is the execution. I know this is supposed to be an allegorical novel and that all circumstances are contrived. I should not expect heroes. But really,</span>
  <span id="freeText9745975458330583730" style="display:none">Bleh. Reading <i>Nothing</i> is like watching a train wreck you know is coming. You know it's going to be depressing as hell, but just you can't look away. <br /><br />I will not deny that the prose--as well as the translation--is excellent. The novel was immensely readable and despite all my insides cringing I had to know what happened next. <br /><br />What I simply don't buy is the execution. I know this is supposed to be an allegorical novel and that all circumstances are contrived. I should not expect heroes. But really, why are these kids so bothered by one existential nutcase in a tree? Why not just let him believe what he wants to believe? Are teenage minds so pliable that at first sign of peer pressure they lose all their basic morality (ahem, killing animals for leisure is never, ever okay) and reasoning skills? I mean, where are the parents? What about the small day-to-today pleasures that this novel leaves out? <br /><br />Whatever. I just don't buy it. It never feels organic, not once, which is its biggest flaw as a work of fiction. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9745975458330583730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

The thing that struck me the most was the deep analysis of human anger and cruelty. It all started as an innocent quest, a group of students decides to convince a classmate that there's some meaning in life. But the things soon get out of hand, the children become wicked, they become thieves, rapers, murderers, they loose their innocence and to me, it was like I was loosing hope in the entire humanity, when even kids, the innocent ones,turn into monsters, it means there is no hope for human kind.</span>

<div id="review_207162814" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207162814" />
    <a title="Stevie Finegan (SableCaught)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4995592-stevie-finegan-sablecaught"><img alt="Stevie Finegan (SableCaught)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306150818p2/4995592.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207162814?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stevie Finegan (SableCaught)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stevie Finegan (SableCaught)" href="/user/show/4995592-stevie-finegan-sablecaught">Stevie Finegan (SableCaught)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207162814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12897915850953676624">Follow the link to watch my video review:<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s-syr7uMi1c'>http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s-syr7...</a><br/><br/>Nothing – Janne Teller<br/><br/>I firmly believe that there are concepts and experiences far too difficult to put into words; this is not to say they cannot be put into stories.<br/><br/>Words are the first level of language and are sufficient for communicating simple ideas, but to consider anything complex we need to move into metaphors, into parables and into stories. (Replaces as you see fit, for your preferred form of art.)<br/><br/>Janne Te</span>
  <span id="freeText12897915850953676624" style="display:none">Follow the link to watch my video review:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s-syr7uMi1c">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=s-syr7...</a><br /><br />Nothing – Janne Teller<br /><br />I firmly believe that there are concepts and experiences far too difficult to put into words; this is not to say they cannot be put into stories.<br /><br />Words are the first level of language and are sufficient for communicating simple ideas, but to consider anything complex we need to move into metaphors, into parables and into stories. (Replaces as you see fit, for your preferred form of art.)<br /><br />Janne Teller's Nothing astounded me because it perfectly managed to represent feelings I thought were far too complicated to explain. More so than any other book I have previously read, it perfectly toys with an idea of nihilism and allows the reader space to explore ideas about depression and despair. <br /><br />Teller begins her story with Pierre Anthony deciding nothing means anything and so taking up residence in a tree. For the rest of her tale Pierre Anthony sits there, eating plums and acting as a nagging doubt to the other children. They cannot go on as normal with their lives, they are unable to ignore this fear that maybe there is no meaning.<br /><br />To combat Pierre Anthony the children begin to create 'the pile of meaning' giving to it those things which mean most to them, however as the amount the possessions mean to them increases, the less each person is willing to give them up and the darker the story gets.<br /><br />This book doesn't claim to offer a cure for what is incurable, or an answer for what is unanswerable, but it does portray more exactly depression and reactions to depression than I ever thought would be possible. Reading this book everyone can find in it their own experiences; it is a book that works with the deeper form of language and has the ability to summon memories and emotions in the reader that chime clear and distinct like a bell - someone else has felt what you have felt.<br /><br />The most interesting part of Nothing to me was the reactions of the other children, their anger at Pierre Anthony, and their persecution of him. Their desperate need to prove that he was wrong, to prove that there was meaning, so that despondency isn't the only option, because his unhappiness threatened their own abilities to be happy and content.<br /><br />There is a quote I love but can only half remember, (a correction in the comments would be appreciated if anyone recognises it). It goes something like 'Great stories either allow us to escape life, or teach us to endure it'. For a long time now this has been how I have divided my reading, into endurance and escapism. There are books which tell of people I want to know and adventures I want to take part in, and then there are books that change my perspective on my own life, that give me a deeper insight into what it is to be human. <br /><br />To me Nothing is definitely one of the latter books, it's not the kind of story where you want to jump in and join the characters, as I've said, the story gets very dark at some points and I would warn you away from it if you're easily unsettled, no, it's a story that makes you question, and consider, and feel.<br /><br />Nothing is honestly an amazing book, the kind of thing I could definitely see put up alongside Lord of the Flies and The Catcher in the Rye as a teenage classic. It's a book I feel I must buy for people, because if they read it they'll better understand me.<br /><br />Thanks to Janne and Strident for my copy, I really appreciate it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12897915850953676624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_146358778" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/146358778" />
    <a title="Ricki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1037651-ricki"><img alt="Ricki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566971210p2/1037651.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/146358778?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ricki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ricki" href="/user/show/1037651-ricki">Ricki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1037651-ricki?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1037651-ricki?shelf=printz">printz</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer146358778" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17768289670223284439">It is very difficult to rate a book that is brilliantly written, but utterly disturbing. I have many books I have to read and review right now, but I stopped my book assignments to read this one after a good friend posted she was currently reading this, and one hour later, she started swearing in her progress comments on GoodReads. <br/><br/>Pierre Anthon walks out of the classroom and climbs a plum tree. He yells down to his fellow classmates, "It's all a waste of time [...] Everything begins only to end</span>
  <span id="freeText17768289670223284439" style="display:none">It is very difficult to rate a book that is brilliantly written, but utterly disturbing. I have many books I have to read and review right now, but I stopped my book assignments to read this one after a good friend posted she was currently reading this, and one hour later, she started swearing in her progress comments on GoodReads. <br /><br />Pierre Anthon walks out of the classroom and climbs a plum tree. He yells down to his fellow classmates, "It's all a waste of time [...] Everything begins only to end. The moment you were born you begin to die. That's how it is with everything."<br /><br />Pierre's classmates want him to come down from the tree, so they set out to create a heap of meaning. They decide to give up something that means the most to them. When they realize that no one will give up their most meaningful possession, they take turns deciding for each other.<br /><br />This chilling allegory made me continually cringe. I was forced to laugh in parts because I couldn't get over how twisted Teller's mind could be. I absolutely think it deserves to be a Printz honor book and Batchelder honor book. Just because something is disturbing doesn't mean it should get a low rating, silly reviewers! There were parts that were unbelievable, but it isn't meant to be taken literally. Also, many of the reviewers seem to have forgotten that this is a translation. Although the characters are thirteen, I think this is intended for high schoolers or adults. <br /><br />When Shirley Jackson's "The Lottery" was released, numerous people canceled their subscriptions to <i>The New Yorker</i>. It was banned from several schools. This book reminds me a lot of that story because it uses uncomfortable plot points to make lasting meaning for the reader.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17768289670223284439" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1875607827" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1875607827" />
    <a title="Maria" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2262926-maria"><img alt="Maria" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420907415p2/2262926.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1875607827?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maria" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maria" href="/user/show/2262926-maria">Maria</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781442441163" href="/book/show/12468831-nothing">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1875607827" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8887401601957720833">Pierre Anthon announced, on his first day as a seventh-grader, that there was no meaning to life. After professing his truth, he abandoned the classroom and found refuge on a plum tree. As his classmates walked by on their way to school, Pierre Anthon reminded them of the lack of meaning in their stride, setting them on a frenzy to prove him wrong.<br/><blockquote>“I’m sitting here in nothing. And better to be sitting in nothing than in something that isn’t anything.”</blockquote><br/><i>Nothing</i> by Janne Teller had me immediately th</span>
  <span id="freeText8887401601957720833" style="display:none">Pierre Anthon announced, on his first day as a seventh-grader, that there was no meaning to life. After professing his truth, he abandoned the classroom and found refuge on a plum tree. As his classmates walked by on their way to school, Pierre Anthon reminded them of the lack of meaning in their stride, setting them on a frenzy to prove him wrong.<br /><blockquote>“I’m sitting here in nothing. And better to be sitting in nothing than in something that isn’t anything.”</blockquote><br /><i>Nothing</i> by Janne Teller had me immediately thinking of <i>Lord of the Flies</i> by William Golding. The mood seems quite similar, but I believe <i>Nothing</i> takes a step further into the abyss. To be quite honest, I am still trying to figure out what exactly that entails.<br /><br />I should start by mentioning the writing style as it’s probably the most tangible thing about this book. There is something incredibly peculiar about it. It feels <i>raw</i>, like over-scrubbed skin, making every single ghost of an emotion grow into something immeasurable. There are also the overpowering silences. Having read the whole book out loud, it is impossible to label them <i>chance</i> or even <i>coincidence</i>. I believe this book was skillfully designed to create a sort of emotional echo that feels claustrophobic at times.<br /><br />That said, I have been meditating about the ending for hours. At first I was disappointed, having expected something groundbreaking to happen, an extraordinary lesson to arise from a middle that had me shuddering. Then I started thinking, and by that I mean definitely <i>over</i>thinking the whole thing. As readers we witnessed these events, we read through them holding onto an ending yet to come, an ending that we expected to deliver hope, <i>meaning</i>. However, just like the children, we encountered none after having deposited <i>everything</i>. Disturbing beyond words, yes, but perhaps not beyond reason. <br /><blockquote>“There was definitely something that mattered in spite of everything, even if that something was something you had to lose.”</blockquote><br />I can’t help but think that Pierre Anthon is partially right. We do tend to forget our role as creators of meaning, setting its weight on time instead, time that has no shoulders to carry it on and no hands to deliver it with. Thought-provoking, is it not?<br /><br />All in all, the only thing I am sure of regarding this book is that it is certainly going to haunt me for a long time.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8887401601957720833" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13118691179910699348">Actual Rating: 3.5 stars<br/>I'm not gonna be sleeping tonight woo.<br/><br/>Okay so this book was a recommendation by the lovely Gabie. I read it because i'd known it is one of her favorites, and I highly trust her book ratings. And though I didn't enjoy this as much as she did, I can still see why people enjoy this although I didn't take particular liking to it too, too much.<br/><br/>I thought I wasn't a sensitive reader anymore, but as I read, I was very disturbed.   <span id="freeText13118691179910699348" style="display:none">Actual Rating: 3.5 stars<br />I'm not gonna be sleeping tonight woo.<br /><br />Okay so this book was a recommendation by the lovely Gabie. I read it because i'd known it is one of her favorites, and I highly trust her book ratings. And though I didn't enjoy this as much as she did, I can still see why people enjoy this although I didn't take particular liking to it too, too much.<br /><br />I thought I wasn't a sensitive reader anymore, but as I read, I was very disturbed. There was a point in the middle of the book where I had to stop and put the book down for 24 hours because it frightened me. I don't think that's ever happened to me in a very long time while reading anything. So I do not recommend this to people who are sensitive to graphic images. <br /><br />Otherwise, the only flaw I thought this book bore was that the translation from Danish to English was a bit faulty. It felt like a lot of things got lost in translation that could have been so much better perceived if it were in Danish. The writing wasn't bad though, so I didn't take off too much from the rating about that. <br /><br />Overall, I thought this was a really deep novel that pushed the boundary between right and wrong, in a really creepy but also strangely realistic way. Someone had compared this to Lord Of The Flies, and I definitely agree.<br /><br />I'm satisfied and this is something that i'll be pondering for a while to come, so I can say it definitely wasn't a waste of my time. </span>
