<div id="review_107656991" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107656991" />
    <a title="Rebecca" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/365896-rebecca"><img alt="Rebecca" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/107656991?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rebecca" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rebecca" href="/user/show/365896-rebecca">Rebecca</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/365896-rebecca?shelf=teen">teen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/365896-rebecca?shelf=interpersonal-relationships-fiction">interpersonal-relationships-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/365896-rebecca?shelf=school-fiction">school-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/365896-rebecca?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/365896-rebecca?shelf=philosophy-fiction">philosophy-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer107656991" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer928339007620481219">Ann purchased this book for the library based on very good reviews by Kirkus, Voya, and SLJ (I think). It was often compared favorably to Lord of the Flies. I didn't like it and think it's overrated. There are books I've read, haven't liked, and yet still felt it was worthy of discussion but this isn't one. A class of Danish 7th graders are motivated to create a "heap of meaning" when one student leaves school after announcing life has no meaning and goes and sits in a plum tree. Every day the c</span>
  <span id="freeText928339007620481219" style="display:none">Ann purchased this book for the library based on very good reviews by Kirkus, Voya, and SLJ (I think). It was often compared favorably to Lord of the Flies. I didn't like it and think it's overrated. There are books I've read, haven't liked, and yet still felt it was worthy of discussion but this isn't one. A class of Danish 7th graders are motivated to create a "heap of meaning" when one student leaves school after announcing life has no meaning and goes and sits in a plum tree. Every day the classmates pass him and his provoking jeers. As their "heap of meaning" grows, so do their demands of the sacrifices each kid must pay. It spirals into, for me, the ridiculous. When the heap becomes public knowledge, the response, especially from the Americans, is laughable and unbelievable. And yet, this is most definitely NOT a funny book. Perhaps its distinctly European flavor was off-putting for me. The Danish Cultural Ministry awarded it their prize for best children's/youth book of 2001. So, between the good reviews and the prizes awarded, apparently I have a minority opinion. But, whereas, Lord of the Flies had an internal logic I could follow (boys, without adult guidance, left to themselves on a deserted island, devolve into savages), this made no sense to me. A boy sits in a plum tree and yells "life has no meaning" so an entire group of children, still living with their parents and attending school in a civilized society, goes slowly berserk and descend into a gruesome mob mentality. I just can't buy the premise so the rest of the book "had no meaning" for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="928339007620481219" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer12238020147302738198">This is an award winning book aimed at a YA audience. It utilizes your typical shock-and-awe-because-I-am-too-lazy-to-tell-a-coherent-story writing technique common to many post-modern authors of note. The idea is to take an idea or theme and hammer it into the ground with brutal violence and offensive imagery, sacrificing authorial integrity and perspective along the way. Yippi skippi.   <span id="freeText12238020147302738198" style="display:none">This is an award winning book aimed at a YA audience. It utilizes your typical shock-and-awe-because-I-am-too-lazy-to-tell-a-coherent-story writing technique common to many post-modern authors of note. The idea is to take an idea or theme and hammer it into the ground with brutal violence and offensive imagery, sacrificing authorial integrity and perspective along the way. Yippi skippi. Listen, I'm totally cool with existential ideas being explored in novels (some of my favorite novels do just that) and I think that young people should struggle with these philosophical ideas as much as all of us should... but COME ON. When an author sacrifices a story and characters and perspective and reality and a whole lot else... well, what is left is mostly NOTHING. And there ya go. This is manipulative and contrived stuff at its finest (or worst)... and, well, it has manipulated a whole lot of folks who think it is a great novel. They are free to their opinion and believe it or not, I think each of our opinions actually have meaning... something this text could never understand.</span>

  <span id="freeText10225670405745538466" style="display:none">I'm not quite sure, how I feel about this book. In some way, it didn't make sense at all, but anyhow, I couldn't put it down. The way this book focused on the meaning of life, was something very special. What is important, and what isn't? And maybe Pierre Anthon is right, maybe life just is nothing at all.     <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1995091696" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1995091696" />
    <a title="JumbleofJargon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39023994-jumbleofjargon"><img alt="JumbleofJargon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542591726p2/39023994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1995091696?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JumbleofJargon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JumbleofJargon" href="/user/show/39023994-jumbleofjargon">JumbleofJargon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39023994-jumbleofjargon?shelf=enlightening-thought-provoking">enlightening-thought-provoking</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39023994-jumbleofjargon?shelf=want-to-buy">want-to-buy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39023994-jumbleofjargon?shelf=good-but-slightly-terrifying">good-but-slightly-terrifying</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39023994-jumbleofjargon?shelf=rereadable">rereadable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39023994-jumbleofjargon?shelf=phenomenal-novels">phenomenal-novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39023994-jumbleofjargon?shelf=my-best-reviews">my-best-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1995091696" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5402699489856828241">I had a good feeling about this from <i>
  <b>page 11</b>
</i> and it certainly did NOT disappoint. <b>I beseech you all to read this !</b> I guess all those books with shiny stickers on them have shiny stickers on them for a reason.<br/><br/><i>What is the meaning of life? Does anything have purpose? Why are we here?</i><br/><br/>These are common questions most humans ponder at some point in their lives. <b>What's odd about Teller's <u>Nothing</u> is that a 7th grader, Pierre-Anthon, has considered these kinds of questions. </b> And this is merely the <u>fir</u></span>
  <span id="freeText5402699489856828241" style="display:none">I had a good feeling about this from <i>
  <b>page 11</b>
</i> and it certainly did NOT disappoint. <b>I beseech you all to read this !</b> I guess all those books with shiny stickers on them have shiny stickers on them for a reason.<br /><br /><i>What is the meaning of life? Does anything have purpose? Why are we here?</i><br /><br />These are common questions most humans ponder at some point in their lives. <b>What's odd about Teller's <u>Nothing</u> is that a 7th grader, Pierre-Anthon, has considered these kinds of questions. </b> And this is merely the <u>first</u> of many oddities the reader notices in <i>Nothing</i>. <b>Sidebar:</b> As I read this, I thought the kids were doing all these things at age 11/12 because that's the age 7th graders are in America, but I'll save you some horror by telling you that in Norway 7th graders are 13/14 years old. A detail I only discovered <b>after</b> I finished the novel and read the bits in the back. The things that occur in this novel are slightly more palatable when you know the students are a bit older. Not just a bit older but at the milestone age of 13/14 - the beginning of adolescence, confusing emotions, hormones and a plethora of other changes. <br /><br />Precocious Pierre-Anthon has concluded what most don't figure out until around their late teens or adulthood. He believes nothing has any meaning. <b>You only work to retire and do nothing then die, so why not start doing nothing now?</b> With that thought, Pierre-Anthon leaves school to sit in a plum tree all day and do nothing, that is if nothing includes harassing his classmates about the meaningless of life. <b>
  <i>The platitudes Pierre-Anthon shouted from his perch in the plum tree set into motion a series of bizarre and ultimately horrifying events.</i>
</b><br /><br />All of Pierre-Anthon's classmates were determined to prove to him that life has meaning and that they're not all wasting their time going to school to work to retire to die. His classmates went to extreme measures to prove this to him. And in the end - you're going to have to read it yourself. But I did not anticipate it AT ALL. <br /><br />This is by far one of the most bizarre things I have ever read. But it is also kind of perfect. There are no clear morals or even any indication from the author as to what is right or wrong. The reader, like the students, has to figure it out for herself or himself. <i>Does life have meaning? Is Pierre-Anthon right? Are the students' actions justified?</i> None of these questions have single, simple, clear answers. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <b> I personally felt the students were SO wrong! I can't believe Sofia sacrificed her innocence! And for what? It frustrated me that they did all these things having no guarantee that Pierre-Anthon would even leave the plum tree to see and come to the sudden arbitrary realization that he had been wrong?! <br /><br />Pierre-Anthon was the catalyst, but I do not consider him to be at fault. Even though much of what he said was true, the students couldn't they see that things that mean something to them may mean nothing to Pierre-Anthon and that is completely okay. It is not their responsibility to adjust Pierre-Anthon's thinking. They acted of their own volition and if things didn't end the way they wanted - that's on them! </b>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />A question I certainly wondered and many other readers might also ponder is: <b>
  <i> Why didn't the students just ignore Pierre-Anthon?</i>
</b> I don't know the author's rationale, so I can only state what I think. If they just ignored Pierre-Anthon, <b>firstly</b>, there would be no novel. <b>Secondly,</b> perhaps the main reason the students cannot ignore Pierre-Anthon is because the things he's saying <b>terrify</b> them. And they've not yet mastered the art of ignoring what's right in front of them as many adults have so they HAVE to prove him wrong. <b>
  <i>As the students try to prove Pierre-Anthon wrong, they are really hoping to confirm for themselves, at the end of the whole ordeal, that their actions are <u>not</u> futile. That Pierre-Anthon is wrong. That everything is not pointless. And that life does indeed have meaning.</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>As Janne Teller expertly brings some of life's monumental questions to the forefront of 7th graders' minds, I think she highlights several important things about society.</b> Perhaps she wrote this in part to highlight the fallacy in education systems teaching Biology, German, and History; but failing to teach students about how to deal with certain emotions and feelings, how to find meaning in life <b>(</b><i>in a healthy way</i><b>)</b>, understanding that different things have different meanings for different people and that this is totally fine. Perhaps Teller aims to point out that even adults themselves have encounter similar difficulties when faced with answer Pierre-Anthon antagonistic remarks. <br /><br />I don't know Janne Teller's motives. I plan on doing copious research on Teller after I write this review. I just wanted to get my initial thoughts down first - unblemished by others' opinions. <b>This is the first book that's made me feel something in a really long time.</b> Even though it was disturbing, I think I loved it. I love it for the questions and discussions it inspires. I feel like I could talk about Sofia alone for hours. I love that it discusses quandaries that most people ignore. Quandaries that I often ponder. I frequently ask myself, especially recently with final exams and all, what motivates people to DO life? Why do people get out of bed in the morning? Why do people do anything at all when they know they're just going to die one day? Whoa, am I Pierre-Anthon? I know I haven't found my <i>"why"</i> yet, but I hope to one day. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Hopefully, without severing any limbs or killing anyone. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><b>Why You Should Read This:</b><br />- It's freaking amazing !<br />- It makes you think.<br />- It's a complex YA novel. <i> Yes folks, it DOES indeed exist. </i><br />- It ignites intriguing discussions.<br />- It's fun to tell people you're reading <i>
  <b>Nothing</b>
</i>, when they ask.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5402699489856828241" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2332955551" />
    <a title="Prince William Public Libraries" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37207761-prince-william-public-libraries"><img alt="Prince William Public Libraries" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576600105p2/37207761.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2332955551?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Prince William Public Libraries" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Prince William Public Libraries" href="/user/show/37207761-prince-william-public-libraries">Prince William Public Libraries</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37207761-prince-william-public-libraries?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37207761-prince-william-public-libraries?shelf=suspense">suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37207761-prince-william-public-libraries?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2332955551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5359475441452471095">What is the meaning of life? Is there a meaning at all? Do the things we love really give us meaning? <br/><br/>Pierre sits perches in a plum tree taunting his classmates, and insisting there's no meaning to life; we're born to die, and that's about it (he's like a demented, annoying little Nietzsche). But his classmates disagree: life has a lot of meaning. They have people, things, experiences, etc. that give them meaning. To prove him wrong, his classmates decide to compile the things that give them mea</span>
  <span id="freeText5359475441452471095" style="display:none">What is the meaning of life? Is there a meaning at all? Do the things we love really give us meaning? <br /><br />Pierre sits perches in a plum tree taunting his classmates, and insisting there's no meaning to life; we're born to die, and that's about it (he's like a demented, annoying little Nietzsche). But his classmates disagree: life has a lot of meaning. They have people, things, experiences, etc. that give them meaning. To prove him wrong, his classmates decide to compile the things that give them meaning into what inevitably becomes "The Heap of Meaning."<br /><br />This Heap renders a lot of attention on a global scale: reporters travel to interview the kids behind it, take pictures etc. The Heap of Meaning reaches infamy.<br /><br />But have they achieved meaning? Do they prove Pierre incorrect in his nihilism? And how far will they go to find meaningful things to place on the heap?<br /><br />This is one of the darkest YA novels I've read, but one of my favorites as well (I'm also partial to anything that's been a Banned Book).<br /><br />-Amanda</span>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FjrlQz0TG5iq2giH5i4QbDMT94xphCsqM1qDVR5wM4yMplY32COBvxfOvtTN4JlptGoYuf9dpleSEWaJC+JVCQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FOCo71OEYDSdSBe1LlI5fnP/u0m9K0XfAhdmiEvrVkOOfBubtrT6EyBcoeYFnLB79IZUfCvyyKKjXINUXnkwxg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/453897.Janne_Teller">About Janne Teller</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<span id="freeTextContainer5994554080028915551">Danish writer and essayist Austro-German origin.<br/>Educated as a macroeconomist, Janne Teller worked for the United Nations and the European Union in resolving conflicts and humanitarian issues around the world, especially in Africa. She began writing fiction full time since 1995. She has lived in various parts of the world, such as Brussels, Paris, Copenhagen, Bangladesh, Tanzania and Mozambique. No</span>
  <span id="freeText5994554080028915551" style="display:none">Danish writer and essayist Austro-German origin.<br />Educated as a macroeconomist, Janne Teller worked for the United Nations and the European Union in resolving conflicts and humanitarian issues around the world, especially in Africa. She began writing fiction full time since 1995. She has lived in various parts of the world, such as Brussels, Paris, Copenhagen, Bangladesh, Tanzania and Mozambique. Now living in New York.<br />Janne Teller's literature, which consists mainly of novels and essays, always focuses on existential outlook on life and human civilization, which causes often controversial debates. Furthermore, it was thought that her work <i>Intet</i> (2000), which was initially banned, revolutionized the novel for youth, and became a worldwide success.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5994554080028915551" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
